<p>Batteries with greatest <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> such as metal-air fuel cells usually cannot be recharged in purely electric way. Instead some kind of metallurgical process is needed, such as aluminum smelting and similar.</p>
<p>Silicon-air, aluminum-air and other metal-air fuel cells look promising candidates for swap batteries.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> Any source of energy, renewable or non-renewable, could be used to remake used metal-air fuel cells with relatively high efficiency. Investment in infrastructure will be needed. The cost of such batteries could be an issue, although they could be made with replaceable anodes and electrolyte.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Chassis_swapping">Chassis swapping</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Chassis swapping">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Chassis swapping" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Instead of replacing batteries, it is possible to replace the entire chassis (including the batteries, electric motor and wheels) of an electric <a href="/wiki/Modular_vehicle" title="Modular vehicle">Modular vehicle</a>.</p>
<p>Such a system was patented in 2000<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup> by Dr Gordon Dower and three road-licensed prototypes have been built by the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://ridek.com">Ridek Corporation</a> in Point Roberts, Washington.</p>
<p>Dr Dower has proposed that an individual might own only the body (or perhaps a few different style bodies) for their vehicle, and would lease the chassis from a pool, thereby reducing the depreciation costs associated with vehicle ownership.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_in-development_technologies">Other in-development technologies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Other in-development technologies">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Other in-development technologies" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Electric_double-layer_capacitor" title="Electric double-layer capacitor">Electric double-layer capacitor</a></div>
<p>Conventional <a href="/wiki/Electric_double-layer_capacitor" title="Electric double-layer capacitor">electric double-layer capacitors</a> are being worked to achieve the energy density of lithium ion batteries, offering almost unlimited lifespans and no environmental issues. High-K electric double-layer capacitors, such as <a href="/wiki/EEStor" title="EEStor">EEStor</a>'s EESU, could improve lithium ion energy density several times over if they can be produced. Lithium-sulphur batteries offer <span class="nowrap">250 Wh/kg</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> Sodium-ion batteries promise <span class="nowrap">400 Wh/kg</span> with only minimal expansion/contraction during charge/discharge and a very high surface area.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> Researchers from one of the Ukrainian state universities claim that they have manufactured samples of pseudocapacitor based on Li-ion intercalation process with <span class="nowrap">318 Wh/kg</span> specific energy, which seem to be at least two times improvement in comparison to typical Li-ion batteries.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Safety">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Safety" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> in <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a> (<a href="/wiki/UNECE" title="UNECE" class="mw-redirect">UNECE</a>) has adopted the first international regulation (Regulation 100) on safety of both fully electric and hybrid electric cars to ensure that cars with a <a href="/wiki/High_voltage" title="High voltage">high voltage</a> electric power train, such as hybrid and fully EVs, are as safe as <a href="/wiki/Combustion_car" title="Combustion car" class="mw-redirect">combustion cars</a>. The EU and Japan have already indicated that they intend to incorporate the new UNECE Regulation in their respective rules on technical standards for vehicles<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>There is a growing concern about the safety of EVs, their charging systems and their <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle_battery" title="Electric vehicle battery">batteries</a>. But EVs must meet all the same safety standards as conventional vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> For <a href="/wiki/Functional_safety" title="Functional safety" class="mw-redirect">functional safety</a> there are already standards available (<a href="/wiki/ISO_26262" title="ISO 26262">ISO 26262</a> and <a href="/wiki/IEC_61508" title="IEC 61508">IEC 61508</a>), as well as for charging systems (UL 2202, UL 2251 or UL Subject 2594). For batteries, chemical and mechanical components certain tests<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> and simulations can be performed to validate and certify their safety.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Highway_Traffic_Safety_Administration" title="National Highway Traffic Safety Administration">National Highway Traffic Safety Administration</a>, a U.S. federal agency, opened a defect investigation on November 25, 2011 after concerns that the <a href="/wiki/Chevy_Volt" title="Chevy Volt" class="mw-redirect">Chevy Volt</a> is at risk of battery fires in a crash. The U.S. House of Representatives is holding a hearing on the matter in January, 2011. Automotive consulting firm <a href="/wiki/CNW_Marketing_Research" title="CNW Marketing Research">CNW Marketing Research</a> discovered a decline in consumer interest in the Volt, citing the fires as having made an impact on consumer perception.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Advocates of EVs are concerned that the focus of the media and the reaction of U.S. Representative Jim Jordan are unwarranted. The fires leading to the investigations occurred only during NHTSA controlled crashes, including simulated rolling of the vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup> In contrast, 184,000 vehicles caught fire on U.S. roads in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Advantages_and_disadvantages_of_EVs">Advantages and disadvantages of EVs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Advantages and disadvantages of EVs">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Advantages and disadvantages of EVs" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental">Environmental</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Environmental">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Environmental" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Due to efficiency of electric engines as compared to combustion engines, even when the electricity used to charge EVs comes from a <a href="/wiki/CO2" title="CO2" class="mw-redirect">CO<sub>2</sub></a>-emitting source, such as a coal- or gas-fired powered plant, the net CO<sub>2</sub> production from an electric car is typically one-half to one-third of that from a comparable combustion vehicle.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>EVs release almost no air pollutants at the place where they are operated. In addition, it is generally easier to build pollution-control systems into centralised power stations than retrofit enormous numbers of cars.</p>
<p>EVs typically have less <a href="/wiki/Noise_pollution" title="Noise pollution">noise pollution</a> than an <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine_vehicle" title="Internal combustion engine vehicle" class="mw-redirect">internal combustion engine vehicle</a>, whether it is at rest or in motion. EVs emit no tailpipe CO<sub>2</sub> or pollutants such as <a href="/wiki/NOx" title="NOx">NOx</a>, <a href="/wiki/NMHC" title="NMHC" class="mw-redirect">NMHC</a>, CO and <a href="/wiki/Particulate" title="Particulate" class="mw-redirect">PM</a> at the point of use.<sup id="cite_ref-ec.europa.eu_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ec.europa.eu-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Electric motors don't require oxygen, unlike <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engines</a>; this is useful for <a href="/wiki/Submarine" title="Submarine">submarines</a> and for <a href="/wiki/Rover_(space_exploration)" title="Rover (space exploration)">space rovers</a>.</p>
<p>While electric and hybrid cars have reduced tailpipe carbon emissions, the energy they consume is sometimes produced by means that have environmental impacts. For example, the majority of <a href="/wiki/Electricity_generation" title="Electricity generation">electricity produced in the United States</a> comes from <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a> (<a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>), so use of an EV in the United States would not be completely <a href="/wiki/Carbon_neutral" title="Carbon neutral" class="mw-redirect">carbon neutral</a>. Electric and hybrid cars can help decrease energy use and pollution, with local no pollution at all being generated by EVs, and may someday use only renewable resources, but the choice that would have the lowest negative environmental impact would be a lifestyle change in favor of walking, biking, use of public transit or <a href="/wiki/Telecommuting" title="Telecommuting">telecommuting</a>. Governments may invest in research and development of electric cars with the intention of reducing the impact on the environment, where they could instead develop pedestrian-friendly communities or electric mass transit.<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanical">Mechanical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Mechanical">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Mechanical" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Electric-van.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Electric-van.jpg/170px-Electric-van.jpg" width="170" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Electric-van.jpg/255px-Electric-van.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Electric-van.jpg/340px-Electric-van.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Electric-van.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
An <a href="/wiki/Alk%C3%A8" title="Alkè">Alkè</a> electric city van.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Electric motors are mechanically very simple.</p>
<p>Electric motors often achieve 90% <a href="/wiki/Energy_conversion_efficiency" title="Energy conversion efficiency">energy conversion efficiency</a><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> over the full range of speeds and power output and can be precisely controlled. They can also be combined with <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_braking" title="Regenerative braking" class="mw-redirect">regenerative braking</a> systems that have the ability to convert movement energy back into stored electricity. This can be used to reduce the wear on brake systems (and consequent brake pad dust) and reduce the total energy requirement of a trip. Regenerative braking is especially effective for start-and-stop city use.</p>
<p>They can be finely controlled and provide high torque from rest, unlike <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engines</a>, and do not need multiple gears to match power curves. This removes the need for <a href="/wiki/Transmission_(mechanics)" title="Transmission (mechanics)">gearboxes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Torque_converter" title="Torque converter">torque converters</a>.</p>
<p>EVs provide quiet and smooth operation and consequently have less noise and <a href="/wiki/Vibration" title="Vibration">vibration</a> than internal combustion engines.<sup id="cite_ref-ec.europa.eu_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ec.europa.eu-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> While this is a desirable attribute, it has also evoked concern that the absence of the usual sounds of an approaching vehicle poses a danger to blind, elderly and very young pedestrians. To mitigate this situation, automakers and individual companies are developing systems that produce <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle_warning_sounds" title="Electric vehicle warning sounds">warning sounds</a> when EVs are moving slowly, up to a speed when normal motion and rotation (road, suspension, electric motor, etc.) noises become audible.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_resilience">Energy resilience</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Energy resilience">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Energy resilience" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Electricity is a form of energy that remains within the country or region where it was produced and can be multi-sourced. As a result it gives the greatest degree of <a href="/wiki/Energy_resilience" title="Energy resilience" class="mw-redirect">energy resilience</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_efficiency">Energy efficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Energy efficiency">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Energy efficiency" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>EV '<a href="/wiki/Tank-to-wheel" title="Tank-to-wheel" class="mw-redirect">tank-to-wheels</a>' efficiency is about a factor of 3 higher than <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine_vehicle" title="Internal combustion engine vehicle" class="mw-redirect">internal combustion engine vehicles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ec.europa.eu_54-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ec.europa.eu-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> Energy is not consumed while the vehicle is stationary, unlike internal combustion engines which consume fuel while idling. However, looking at the <a href="/wiki/Well-to-wheel" title="Well-to-wheel" class="mw-redirect">well-to-wheel</a> efficiency of EVs, their total emissions, while still lower, are closer to an efficient gasoline or diesel in most countries where electricity generation relies on fossil fuels.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>It is worth noting that well-to-wheel efficiency of an EV has far less to do with the vehicle itself and more to do with the method of electricity production. A particular EV would instantly become twice as efficient if electricity production were switched from fossil fuel to a wind or tidal primary source of energy. Thus when "well-to-wheels" is cited, one should keep in mind that the discussion is no longer about the vehicle, but rather about the entire energy supply infrastructure - in the case of fossil fuels this should also include energy spent on exploration, mining, refining, and distribution.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cost_of_recharge">Cost of recharge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Cost of recharge">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Cost of recharge" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>According to General Motors, as reported by CNN Money, the GM Volt will cost "less than purchasing a cup of your favorite coffee" to recharge. The Volt should cost less than 2 cents per mile to drive on electricity, compared with 12 cents a mile on gasoline at a price of $3.60 a gallon. This means a trip from Los Angeles to New York would cost $56 on electricity, and $336 with gasoline. This would be the equivalent to paying 60 cents a gallon of gas.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The reality is that the cost of operating an EV varies wildly depending on the part of the world in which the owner lives. In some locations an EV costs less to drive than a comparable gas-powered vehicle, as long as the higher initial purchase-price is not factored in (i.e. a pure comparison of gasoline cost to electricity cost). In the USA, however, in states which have a tiered electricity rate schedule, "fuel" for EVs today costs owners significantly more than fuel for a comparable gas-powered vehicle. A study done by Purdue University found that in California most users already reach the third pricing tier for electricity each month, and adding an EV could push them into the fourth or fifth (highest, most expensive) tier, meaning that they will be paying in excess of $.45 cents per KWH for electricity to recharge their vehicle. At this price, which is higher than the average electricity price in the US, it is dramatically more expensive to drive a pure-EV than it is to drive a traditional pure-gas powered vehicle. "The objective of a tiered pricing system is to discourage consumption. It's meant to get you to think about turning off your lights and conserving electricity. In California, the unintended consequence is that plug-in hybrid cars won't be economical under this system," said Tyner (the author), whose findings were published in the online version of the journal Energy Policy.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stabilization_of_the_grid">Stabilization of the grid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Stabilization of the grid">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Stabilization of the grid" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Since EVs can be plugged into the <a href="/wiki/Electric_grid" title="Electric grid" class="mw-redirect">electric grid</a> when not in use, there is a potential for battery powered vehicles to even out the demand for electricity by feeding electricity <i>into</i> the grid from their batteries during peak use periods (such as midafternoon air conditioning use) while doing most of their charging at night, when there is unused generating capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Vehicle-to-grid" title="Vehicle-to-grid">vehicle-to-grid</a> (V2G) connection has the potential to reduce the need for new power plants, as long as vehicle owners do not mind their batteries being drained during the day by the power company prior to needing to use their vehicle for a return-commute home in the evening.</p>
<p>Furthermore, our current electricity infrastructure may need to cope with increasing shares of variable-output power sources such as windmills and PV solar panels. This variability could be addressed by adjusting the speed at which EV batteries are charged, or possibly even discharged.</p>
<p>Some concepts see battery exchanges and battery charging stations, much like gas/petrol stations today. Clearly these will require enormous storage and charging potentials, which could be manipulated to vary the rate of charging, and to output power during shortage periods, much as diesel generators are used for short periods to stabilize some national grids.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Range">Range</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Range">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Range" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Many electric designs have limited range, due to the low energy density of batteries compared to the fuel of internal combustion engined vehicles. EVs also often have long recharge times compared to the relatively fast process of refueling a tank. This is further complicated by the current scarcity of public charging stations. "<a href="/wiki/Range_anxiety" title="Range anxiety">Range anxiety</a>" is a label for consumer concern about EV range.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Heating_of_EVs">Heating of EVs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Heating of EVs">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Heating of EVs" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In cold climates, considerable energy is needed to heat the interior of a vehicle and to defrost the windows. With internal combustion engines, this heat already exists as waste combustion heat diverted from the engine cooling circuit. This process offsets the <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a>' external costs. If this is done with battery EVs, the interior heating requires extra energy from the vehicles' batteries. Although some heat could be harvested from the motor(s) and battery, their greater efficiency means there is not as much waste heat available as from a <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">combustion engine</a>.</p>
<p>However, for vehicles which are connected to the grid, battery EVs can be preheated, or cooled, with little or no need for battery energy, especially for short trips.</p>
<p>Newer designs are focused on using super-<a href="/wiki/Thermal_insulation" title="Thermal insulation">insulated</a> cabins which can heat the vehicle using the body heat of the passengers. This is not enough, however, in colder climates as a driver delivers only about 100 W of heating power. A reversible AC-system, cooling the cabin during summer and heating it during winter, seems to be the most practical and promising way of solving the thermal management of the EV. Ricardo Arboix<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> introduced (2008) a new concept based on the principle of combining the thermal-management of the EV-battery with the thermal-management of the cabin using a reversible AC-system. This is done by adding a third heat-exchanger, thermally connected with the battery-core, to the traditional heat pump/air conditioning system used in previous EV-models like the GM EV1 and Toyota RAV4 EV. The concept has proven to bring several benefits, such as prolonging the life-span of the battery as well as improving the performance and overall energy-efficiency of the EV.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Electric_public_transit_efficiency">Electric public transit efficiency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Electric public transit efficiency">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Electric public transit efficiency" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Shifts from private to <a href="/wiki/Public_transport" title="Public transport">public transport</a> (train, <a href="/wiki/Trolleybus" title="Trolleybus">trolleybus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Personal_rapid_transit" title="Personal rapid transit">personal rapid transit</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tram" title="Tram">tram</a>) have the potential for large gains in efficiency in terms of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Individual_mile_per_kWH&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Individual mile per kWH (page does not exist)">individual miles per kWh</a>.</p>
<p>Research shows people do prefer trams,<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> because they are quieter and more comfortable and perceived as having higher status.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Therefore, it may be possible to cut liquid fossil fuel consumption in cities through the use of electric trams.</p>
<p>Trams may be the most energy-efficient form of public transportation, with rubber wheeled vehicles using 2/3 more energy than the equivalent tram, and run on electricity rather than fossil fuels.</p>
<p>In terms of <a href="/wiki/Net_present_value" title="Net present value">net present value</a>, they are also the cheapest—<a href="/wiki/Blackpool_tram" title="Blackpool tram" class="mw-redirect">Blackpool trams</a> are still running after 100-years,<sup class="Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> but combustion buses only last about 15-years.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Incentives_and_promotion">Incentives and promotion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Incentives and promotion">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Incentives and promotion" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="rellink">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Government_incentives_for_plug-in_electric_vehicles" title="Government incentives for plug-in electric vehicles">Government incentives for plug-in electric vehicles</a></div>
<div class="rellink">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_battery_electric_vehicles" title="List of battery electric vehicles" class="mw-redirect">List of battery electric vehicles</a>,&#160;<a href="/wiki/List_of_hybrid_vehicles" title="List of hybrid vehicles">list of hybrid vehicles</a>,&#160;and&#160;<a href="/wiki/List_of_modern_production_plug-in_electric_vehicles" title="List of modern production plug-in electric vehicles">list of modern production plug-in electric vehicles</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: United States">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: United States" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chevy_Volt_%26_Nissan_Leaf_03.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Chevy_Volt_%26_Nissan_Leaf_03.jpg/220px-Chevy_Volt_%26_Nissan_Leaf_03.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Chevy_Volt_%26_Nissan_Leaf_03.jpg/330px-Chevy_Volt_%26_Nissan_Leaf_03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Chevy_Volt_%26_Nissan_Leaf_03.jpg/440px-Chevy_Volt_%26_Nissan_Leaf_03.jpg 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chevy_Volt_%26_Nissan_Leaf_03.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Volt" title="Chevrolet Volt">Chevrolet Volt</a> <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid" title="Plug-in hybrid">plug-in hybrid</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Nissan_Leaf" title="Nissan Leaf">Nissan Leaf</a> <a href="/wiki/Electric_car" title="Electric car">electric car</a> were the first two <a href="/wiki/Mass_production" title="Mass production">mass production</a> <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_electric_vehicle" title="Plug-in electric vehicle">plug-in electric vehicles</a> introduced in the U.S., both in December 2010</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_electric_vehicles_in_the_United_States" title="Plug-in electric vehicles in the United States">Plug-in electric vehicles in the United States</a></div>
<p>President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> has announced $2.4 billion for EVs;<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> $1.5 billion in grants to U.S. based manufacturers to produce highly efficient batteries and their components; up to $500 million in grants to U.S. based manufacturers to produce other components needed for EVs, such as electric motors and other components; and up to $400 million to demonstrate and evaluate Plug-In Hybrids and other electric infrastructure concepts—like <a href="/wiki/Truck_stop" title="Truck stop">truck stop</a> charging station, <a href="/wiki/Electric_rail" title="Electric rail" class="mw-redirect">electric rail</a>, and training for technicians to build and repair EVs (<a href="/wiki/Greencollar_job" title="Greencollar job" class="mw-redirect">greencollar jobs</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Qualifying EVs purchased new are eligible for a one-time federal tax credit that equals 10% of the cost of the vehicle up to $4,000, provided under Section 179A of the <a href="/wiki/Energy_Policy_Act_of_1992" title="Energy Policy Act of 1992">Energy Policy Act of 1992</a>; it was extended through 2007 by the Working Families Tax Relief Act of 2004. A <a href="/wiki/Tax_deduction" title="Tax deduction">tax deduction</a> of up to $100,000 per location is available for qualified EV recharging property used in a trade or business.</p>
<p>In 2008, San Francisco Mayor <a href="/wiki/Gavin_Newsom" title="Gavin Newsom">Gavin Newsom</a>, San Jose Mayor <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Reed" title="Chuck Reed">Chuck Reed</a> and Oakland Mayor <a href="/wiki/Ron_Dellums" title="Ron Dellums">Ron Dellums</a> announced a nine-step policy plan for transforming the Bay Area into the "Electric Vehicle (EV) Capital of the U.S."<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> Other local and state governments have also expressed interest in electric cars.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In March 2009, as part of the <a href="/wiki/American_Recovery_and_Reinvestment_Act" title="American Recovery and Reinvestment Act" class="mw-redirect">American Recovery and Reinvestment Act</a>, the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Energy" title="U.S. Department of Energy" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Department of Energy</a> announced the release of two competitive solicitations for up to $2 billion in federal funding for competitively awarded cost-shared agreements for manufacturing of advanced <a href="/wiki/Battery_(electricity)" title="Battery (electricity)">batteries</a> and related drive components as well as up to $400 million for <a href="/wiki/Transportation_electrification" title="Transportation electrification" class="mw-redirect">transportation electrification</a> demonstration and deployment projects. This announcement will also help meet President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a>'s goal of putting one million <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid_vehicle" title="Plug-in hybrid vehicle" class="mw-redirect">plug-in hybrid vehicles</a> on the road by 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/American_Clean_Energy_and_Security_Act" title="American Clean Energy and Security Act">American Clean Energy and Security Act</a> (ACES), which passed the Energy and Commerce Committee on May 21, 2009, has extensive provisions for electric cars. The bill calls for all electric utilities to, "develop a plan to support the use of plug-in electric drive vehicles, including heavy-duty hybrid electric vehicles". The bill also provides for "smart grid integration," allowing for more efficient, effective delivery of electricity to accommodate the additional demands of plug-in EVs. Finally, the bill allows for the Department of Energy to fund projects that support the development of EV and smart grid technology and infrastructure.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The House of Representatives passed legislation in late 2008, enumerating tax credits ranging from $2500 to $7500 for EV buyers. The actual credit varies depending on the specified vehicle's battery capacity. The Chevrolet Volt and the Tesla vehicles are eligible for the full $7500 credit. The bill called for the credit to be applicable for the first 250,000 vehicles sold per manufacturer.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> The credits were passed in 2008 but went into effect on January 1, 2009, and can be currently used on the Tesla all-electric models.<sup id="cite_ref-energystar.gov_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-energystar.gov-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> The Volt, plug-in Prius, and other PHEVs and BEVs will also be eligible for the credit when they are released in the coming years. The new credits update incentives introduced in 2006, that offered credits for gas-electric hybrids, "Based on a formula determined by vehicle weight, technology, and fuel economy compared to base year models", which expired after 60,000 units per manufacturer.<sup id="cite_ref-energystar.gov_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-energystar.gov-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> The new credits will only apply to plug-in EVs and all-electric vehicles.</p>
<p>A 2013 study published in the journal <i>Energy Policy</i> explored the relative benefits of a vehicle-charging network and hybrid vehicles with larger batteries. Across the battery-capacity and charging-infrastructure scenarios examined, the lowest-cost solution is for more drivers to switch to traditional hybrid electrics or low-capacity plug-in hybrid electric vehicles (PHEVs). Installing charging infrastructure would provide lower gasoline savings per dollar spent than paying for increased PHEV battery capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-JR-ec_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JR-ec-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EP-ec_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EP-ec-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In addition, the study determined that current federal subsidies are "not aligned with the goal of decreased gasoline consumption in a consistent and efficient manner."<sup id="cite_ref-JR-ec_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JR-ec-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EP-ec_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EP-ec-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> For example, hybrid-vehicle credit is given according to battery capacity rather than electric-only vehicle range. This has in part encouraged the creation and marketing of vehicles such as the hybrid <a href="/wiki/Cadillac_Escalade" title="Cadillac Escalade">Cadillac Escalade</a>, which gets a maximum of 23mpg on the highway.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Union">European Union</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: European Union">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: European Union" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Electrification_of_transport" title="Electrification of transport" class="mw-redirect">Electrification of transport</a> (<a href="/wiki/Electromobility" title="Electromobility" class="mw-redirect">electromobility</a>) figures prominently in the Green Car Initiative (GCI),<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> included in the European Economic Recovery Plan. <a href="/wiki/Directorate-General_for_Mobility_and_Transport_(European_Commission)" title="Directorate-General for Mobility and Transport (European Commission)">DG TREN</a> is supporting a large European "electromobility" project on EVs and related infrastructure with a total budget of around €50-million as part of the Green Car Initiative.<sup id="cite_ref-ec.europa.eu_54-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ec.europa.eu-54"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>There are measures to promote <a href="/w/index.php?title=Efficient_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Efficient vehicle (page does not exist)">efficient vehicles</a> in the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Directive_2009/33/EC&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Directive 2009/33/EC (page does not exist)">Directive 2009/33/EC</a> of the European Parliament and of the Council of 23 April 2009 on the promotion of clean and energy-efficient road transport vehicles<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> and in the Directive 2006/32/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 5 April 2006 on <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">energy end-use efficiency</a> and energy services.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/AVERE" title="AVERE" class="mw-redirect">AVERE</a> has a table summarizing the taxation and incentives for these vehicles in the different European countries, related to <a href="/wiki/Subsidy" title="Subsidy">state subsidies</a>, reduction of <a href="/wiki/VAT" title="VAT" class="mw-redirect">VAT</a> and other taxes, insurance facilities, parking and charging facilities (including free recharging on street or in the parking areas), EVs imposed by law and banned circulation for <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_car" title="Petroleum car" class="mw-redirect">petroleum cars</a>, permission to use bus lanes, free <a href="/wiki/Road_tax" title="Road tax">road tax</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toll_free" title="Toll free" class="mw-redirect">toll free</a> travel on highways, exemption from <a href="/wiki/Congestion_charging" title="Congestion charging" class="mw-redirect">congestion charging</a>, free or reduced parking rates, and free charging at <a href="/wiki/Charge_point" title="Charge point" class="mw-redirect">charge points</a>, amongst other initiatives.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> In Denmark, petrol cars are taxed at 180% + 25%, however, EV cars (max. 2000&#160;kg total weight) are only taxed at 25%. Free parking is also offered to EVs in Copenhagen and other cities, and there is free recharging at some parking spaces.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="EU_member_states">EU member states</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: EU member states">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: EU member states" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Republic_of_Ireland">Republic of Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Republic of Ireland">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Republic of Ireland" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>In the Republic of Ireland, in 2010, then Green Party minister for Energy, <a href="/wiki/Eamon_Ryan" title="Eamon Ryan">Eamon Ryan</a> announced a scheme<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> to deploy 1,500 electrical recharging stations for use with EVs. In addition, 30 high voltage fast charging units will be deployed, providing a high speed recharge facility every 60&#160;km on interurban routes. Electricity supplied from these recharging points will be free initially. Additional incentives towards the purchase of EVs were announced, including a €5,000 capital grant. Series production EVs have been exempted from <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_registration_tax_(Ireland)" title="Vehicle registration tax (Ireland)">VRT</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> Annual motor tax for EVs is €104. The Government has set a target of 10% for all vehicles on Irish roads to be electric by 2020.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Finland">Finland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Finland">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Finland" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The Prime Minister of Finland (2003–2010) Mr. <a href="/wiki/Matti_Vanhanen" title="Matti Vanhanen">Matti Vanhanen</a> has mentioned that he wants to see more <a href="/wiki/Electric_car" title="Electric car">electric cars</a> on Finnish roads as soon as possible<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> and with any cost to the governmental car related tax incomes.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> Charging at home from motor and cabin heating outlets (common in all Nordic countries) has been determined to be a possible load on the grid, although this load is expected to mainly take place at night when overall demand is lower. If all cars in Finland run totally on electricity, it will add 7-9 TWh annually to the load, which corresponds to 10% of Finland's annual consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> On-line route planners like <a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="http://www.uppladdning.nu/">http://www.uppladdning.nu/</a> list a daily growing number of free charging outlets set up by merchants and private individuals, making it possible to drive an EV for free from Helsinki through Sweden all the way to Copenhagen.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Denmark">Denmark</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Denmark">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Denmark" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Denmark was planning to introduce a greater number of battery driven electric cars on the streets&#160;— charged on renewable energy from the country's many wind turbines&#160;— ahead of the <a href="/wiki/UN_Climate_Summit" title="UN Climate Summit" class="mw-redirect">UN Climate Summit</a> that descended on Copenhagen in December 2009. A great deal of the electricity is generated by <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbines" title="Wind turbines" class="mw-redirect">wind turbiness</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-euobserver.com_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-euobserver.com-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Germany">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Germany" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Opel_Ampera_and_smart-ed_2012.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Opel_Ampera_and_smart-ed_2012.JPG/220px-Opel_Ampera_and_smart-ed_2012.JPG" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Opel_Ampera_and_smart-ed_2012.JPG/330px-Opel_Ampera_and_smart-ed_2012.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Opel_Ampera_and_smart-ed_2012.JPG/440px-Opel_Ampera_and_smart-ed_2012.JPG 2x" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Opel_Ampera_and_smart-ed_2012.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.22wmf13/skins/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Smart_ED" title="Smart ED" class="mw-redirect">Smart ED</a> <a href="/wiki/All-electric_car" title="All-electric car" class="mw-redirect">all-electric car</a> (right) and <a href="/wiki/Opel_Ampera" title="Opel Ampera" class="mw-redirect">Opel Ampera</a> <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid" title="Plug-in hybrid">plug-in hybrid</a> (left) in Germany</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/National_Electric_Mobility_Platform" title="National Electric Mobility Platform" class="mw-redirect">National Electric Mobility Platform</a>" (<a href="/wiki/NEMP" title="NEMP" class="mw-redirect">NEMP</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> is a German government initiative to develop Germany into a leading market for <a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_mobility&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Electric mobility (page does not exist)">electric mobility</a>, with about 1 million EVs on its streets by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As the latest development (October 2010) DBM Energy's electric Audi A2 completes record setting 372-mile (599&#160;km) drive on a single charge.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Portugal">Portugal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Portugal">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Portugal" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>The Portuguese Government launched in early 2008 a national Programme for Electric Mobility called <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mobi.E&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Mobi.E (page does not exist)">Mobi.E</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>MOBI.E is based on an innovative approach to electric mobility. It has an open-access and market-oriented philosophy and, as a result, it proposes a fully integrated and totally interoperable system, multi-retailer and multi-operator model. Rather than a local experience, Mobi.E is deploying a national electric mobility system. However, the system was designed to be scalable and used in multiple geographies, overcoming the current situation of lack of communication among the different electric mobility experiences that are being deployed in Europe.</p>
<p>Mobi.E allows any individual the access to any provider of electricity in any charging point explored by any service operator. This ensures transparency, low entry barriers and competition along the value chain, with the goal of attracting private investors and benefiting the users, contributing to a faster expansion of the system.</p>
<p>Therefore, Portugal is one of the first countries in the world to have an integrated policy for electric mobility and a national charging network for EVs. By the first semester of 2011, a wide public network of 1 300 normal and 50 fast charging points will be fully implemented in the main 25 cities of the country, thus allowing EV users the ability to travel throughout the country in all comfort and safety.</p>
<p>In the top of the system there is a “Managing Authority” which acts as a Clearing House and intermediates the financial, information and energy flows among users, electricity sellers, operators of charging points, and the providers of any other associated service.</p>
<p>Additionally, several measures were taken to increase the demand for EVs in Portugal: (1) EVs are fully exempt from both the Vehicle Tax due upon purchase (Imposto Sobre Veículos) and the annual Circulation Tax (Imposto Único de Circulação); (2) Personal Income Tax provides an allowance of EUR 803 upon the purchase of EVs; (3) EVs are fully exempt from the 5%-10% company car tax rates which are part of the Corporation Income Tax; (4) The Budget Law provides for an increase of the depreciation costs related to the purchase of EVs for the purpose of Corporation Income Tax; (5) the first 5,000 EVs to be sold in Portugal will receive a 5,000€ incentive fund, and the Cash-for-Clunkers program grants an additional 1,500€ fund if an internal combustion engine vehicle built before 2000 is delivered when acquiring the new EV; (6) The Portuguese State did also commit to play a pedagogic role and defined that EVs will have a 20% share of the annual renewal of public car fleet, starting in 2011.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Spain">Spain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Spain">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Spain" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<div class="Bug6200">Electric vehicles are the future and the driver of the industrial revolution</div>
<div class="templatequotecite">—<a href="/wiki/Miguel_Sebasti%C3%A1n" title="Miguel Sebastián" class="mw-redirect">Miguel Sebastián</a>, Spanish Industry Minister<sup id="cite_ref-blogs.edmunds.com_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blogs.edmunds.com-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Spain's government aims to have 1 million electric cars on the roads by 2014 as part of a plan to cut energy consumption and dependence on expensive imports, Industry Minister Miguel Sebastián said.<sup id="cite_ref-euobserver.com_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-euobserver.com-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-blogs.edmunds.com_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blogs.edmunds.com-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom">United Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: United Kingdom">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: United Kingdom" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<dl>
<dt>Plug-in Car Grant</dt>
</dl>
<p>The Plug-in Car Grant started on 1 January 2011 and is available across the <a href="/wiki/U.K." title="U.K." class="mw-redirect">U.K.</a> The program reduces the up-front cost of eligible cars by providing a 25% grant towards the cost of new plug-in cars capped at <span class="nowrap">GB£5,000</span> (<span style="white-space: nowrap">US$7,800</span>). Both private and business fleet buyers are eligible for this grant which is received at the point of purchase. The subsidy programme is managed in a similar way to the grant made as part of the 2009 <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_scrappage_scheme_(United_Kingdom)" title="Vehicle scrappage scheme (United Kingdom)">Car Scrappage Scheme</a>, allowing consumers to buy an eligible car discounted at the point of purchase with the subsidy claimed back by the manufacturer afterwards.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GCC0210_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCC0210-100"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DfT_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DfT-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The scheme was first announced in January 2009 by the Labour Government. The coalition government, led by <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a>, took office in May 2010 and confirmed their support of the grant on 28 July 2010. This confirmed that <span class="nowrap">GB£43</span> million would be available for the first 15 months of the scheme, with the 2011 Spending Review confirming funding for the programme for the lifetime of the Parliament of around <span class="nowrap">GB£300</span> million.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As of September 2012<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the following cars are eligible for the grant: <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_i-MiEV" title="Mitsubishi i-MiEV">Mitsubishi i-MiEV</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peugeot_iOn" title="Peugeot iOn" class="mw-redirect">Peugeot iOn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Citroen_C-ZERO" title="Citroen C-ZERO" class="mw-redirect">Citroen C-ZERO</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smart_Fortwo_electric_drive" title="Smart Fortwo electric drive" class="mw-redirect">Smart Fortwo electric drive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nissan_Leaf" title="Nissan Leaf">Nissan Leaf</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tata_Indica_Vista_EV" title="Tata Indica Vista EV" class="mw-redirect">Tata Vista</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vauxhall_Ampera" title="Vauxhall Ampera" class="mw-redirect">Vauxhall Ampera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Volt" title="Chevrolet Volt">Chevrolet Volt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Prius_Plug-in_Hybrid" title="Toyota Prius Plug-in Hybrid">Toyota Prius Plug-in Hybrid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renault_Fluence_ZE" title="Renault Fluence ZE" class="mw-redirect">Renault Fluence ZE</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mia_electric" title="Mia electric">Mia electric</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DfT_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DfT-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> As of 30 June 2012, 1,706 claims had been made through the Plug-in Car Grant scheme.<sup id="cite_ref-DfT_101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DfT-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Plug-in Van Grant</dt>
</dl>
<p>The Plug-In Car Grant began in February 2012. Van buyers can receive 20% - up to £8000 - off the cost of a plug-in van. To be eligible for the scheme, vans have to meet performance criteria to ensure safety, range, and ultra-low tailpipe emissions. Consumers, both business and private will receive the discount at the point of purchase.<sup id="cite_ref-UKPIVan_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKPIVan-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As of June 30, 2012, a total of 99 claims have been made through the Plug-in Van Grant scheme. As of September 2012<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the following vans are eligible for the grant: <a href="/wiki/Azure_Transit_Connect_Electric" title="Azure Transit Connect Electric">Azure Transit Connect Electric</a>; <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz_Vito_E-Cell" title="Mercedes-Benz Vito E-Cell" class="mw-redirect">Mercedes-Benz Vito E-Cell</a>; <a href="/w/index.php?title=Faam_Ecomile&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Faam Ecomile (page does not exist)">Faam Ecomile</a>; <a href="/w/index.php?title=Faam_Jolly_2000&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Faam Jolly 2000 (page does not exist)">Faam Jolly 2000</a>; <a href="/wiki/Mia_electric" title="Mia electric">Mia U</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Smith_Electric_Edison" title="Smith Electric Edison" class="mw-redirect">Smith Electric Edison</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-UKPIVan_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UKPIVan-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Plugged-in Places</dt>
</dl>
<p>The Government is supporting the ‘Plugged-In Places’ programme to install vehicle recharging points across the UK. The scheme offers match-funding to consortia of businesses and public sector partners to support the installation of EV recharging infrastructure in lead places across the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-DfT2_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DfT2-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> There are eight Plugged-In Places:East of England;<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> Greater Manchester; London;<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup> Midlands;<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> Milton Keynes;<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> North East;<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> Northern Ireland;<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> and Scotland. The Government also published an Infrastructure Strategy in June 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="China">China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: China">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: China" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_in_China" title="Renewable energy in China">Renewable energy in China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Automobile_industry_in_China" title="Automobile industry in China" class="mw-redirect">Automobile industry in China</a></div>
<p>Many EV companies are looking to China as the leader of future electric car implementation around the world. In April 2009, Chinese officials announced their plan to make China the world's largest producer of electric cars. The <a href="/wiki/Renault-Nissan_Alliance" title="Renault-Nissan Alliance" class="mw-redirect">Renault-Nissan Alliance</a> will work with China's <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Industry_and_Information_Technology" title="Ministry of Industry and Information Technology" class="mw-redirect">Ministry of Industry and Information Technology</a> (MITI) to help set up <a href="/wiki/Battery_recharging" title="Battery recharging" class="mw-redirect">battery recharging</a> networks throughout the <a href="/wiki/Wuhan" title="Wuhan">city of Wuhan</a>, the pilot city in the country's electrical vehicle pilot program. The corporation plans to have EVs on the market by 2011. According to an April 10, 2009 <i>New York Times</i> article entitled "China Outlines Plans for Making Electric Cars" auto manufacturers will possess the opportunity to successfully market their cars to Chinese consumers due to the short and slow commutes that characterize <a href="/wiki/Transport_in_the_People%27s_Republic_of_China" title="Transport in the People's Republic of China" class="mw-redirect">Chinese transportation</a>, and many first time car-buyers are less accustomed to the power of gasoline-powered cars, subsequently diminishing the hindering nature of lower powered EVs. It reports that China would like to assist the industry with automotive innovation by launching a program that worths as much as 10 billion yuan ($1.46 billion). In the same article, it also reports that the U.S. government is providing $25 billion to help cover domestic automobile makers’ research costs.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 2010, it is reported that China, aiming to improve air quality and reduce reliance on fossil fuels, is going to commence a two-year pilot program of subsidizing buyers of alternative- energy cars in the five cities: Shanghai, Changchun, Shenzhen, Hangzhou and Hefei. The subsidy will be as much as 60,000 yuan for battery electric cars and 50,000 yuan ($7,320) for plug-in hybrids. In 2009, BYD delivered 48 F3DM plug-in hybrids in the country. China also plans to expand a project of encouraging the use of energy-efficient and alternative-energy vehicles in public transport to 20 cities from 13.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> The chief executive of Nissan Carlos Ghosn said earlier that the auto maker would likely produce the Leaf, a battery EV, in China if there are "substantial" purchase incentives offered to buyers.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 2011, only 8,159 electric cars were sold in China despite a 120,000 yuan subsidy. Unsubsidized lead-acid EVs are produced without government approval at a rate of more than 30,000 per year in <a href="/wiki/Shandong" title="Shandong">Shandong</a> and requires no driving license because the top speed is less than 50&#160;km/h. They cost 31,600 yuan and have been the target of criticism from major car manufacturers.<sup id="cite_ref-reuShi_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reuShi-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Japan">Japan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Japan">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Japan" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In June 2009, it is reported that consumers in Japan who purchase an EV like <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_i-MiEV" title="Mitsubishi i-MiEV">i-MiEV</a> from Mitsubishi can receive subsidies that reduce cost of the vehicle to 3.209 million yen(about $33,000), down 30% from the original price of 4.59 million yen ($47,560). At that time, it is reported the program runs from April 2009 to March 2010. Electric cars, as well as hybrids, are also exempt from taxes for three years in Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="South_Africa">South Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: South Africa">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: South Africa" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Optimal_Energy_Joule" title="Optimal Energy Joule">Joule</a>, designed by <a href="/wiki/Cape_Town" title="Cape Town">Cape Town</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Optimal_Energy" title="Optimal Energy" class="mw-redirect">Optimal Energy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> made its debut at the 2008 <a href="/wiki/Paris_Motor_Show" title="Paris Motor Show">Paris Motor Show</a>, has a maximum driving range of 300&#160;km. It accommodates two large-cell <a href="/wiki/Lithium_ion" title="Lithium ion" class="mw-redirect">lithium ion</a> battery packs.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=GridCars&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="GridCars (page does not exist)">GridCars</a>, Is a <a href="/wiki/Pretoria" title="Pretoria">Pretoria</a> based company promoting Commuter Cars, their launch vehicle is based on the TREV from Australia. The idea is to build ultra-light EVs, placing less demand on battery requirements, and making the vehicle more affordable.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Buying_and_leasing">Buying and leasing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Buying and leasing">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Buying and leasing" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._Air_Force">U.S. Air Force</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: U.S. Air Force">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: U.S. Air Force" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Air Force officials unveiled a plan Aug. 31, 2011, to establish <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Air_Force_Base" title="Los Angeles Air Force Base">Los Angeles Air Force Base</a>, Calif., as the first federal facility to replace 100 percent of its general purpose fleet with plug-in EVs.</p>
<p>"With gas prices rising and the cost of batteries falling, now is the time to move toward electric vehicles," said Undersecretary of the Air Force Erin Conaton. "The 100-percent Electric Vehicle Base initiative is a critical first step in this direction and will help guide the way for broader fleet electrification."</p>
<p>Initial planning for the installation of charging infrastructure at Los Angeles AFB is already underway, and the vehicles could be in place as soon as January 2012.</p>
<p>As part of the program, all Air Force-owned and -leased general purpose fleet vehicles on the base will be replaced with PEVs. There are approximately 40 eligible vehicles, ranging from passenger sedans to two-ton trucks and shuttle buses. The replacement PEVs include fully -electric, plug-in hybrid electric, and extended-range EVs.</p>
<p>The initiative would not include force protection, tactical and emergency response vehicles, which would remain on an exempt status, according to officials. The program is also subject to environmental review.</p>
<p>Electrification of Los Angeles AFB's general purpose fleet is the first implementation step in an ongoing Department of Defense effort to establish strategies for large-scale integration of PEVs.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._Army">U.S. Army</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: U.S. Army">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: U.S. Army" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/U.S._Army" title="U.S. Army" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Army</a> has announced that it will lease 4,000 <a href="/wiki/Neighborhood_Electric_Vehicle" title="Neighborhood Electric Vehicle">Neighborhood Electric Vehicles</a> (NEVs) within three years. The Army plans to use NEVs at its bases for transporting people around the base, as well as for security patrols and maintenance and delivery services. The Army accepted its first six NEVs at Virginia's <a href="/wiki/Fort_Myer" title="Fort Myer">Fort Myer</a> in March 2009 and will lease a total of 600 NEVs through the rest of the year, followed by the leasing of 1,600 NEVs for each of the following two years. With a full eight-hour recharge, the NEVs can travel 30 miles (48&#160;km) at a top speed of 25&#160;mph (40&#160;km/h).<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_Electric">General Electric</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: General Electric">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: General Electric" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On November 11, 2010, <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a> (GE) announced its plans to buy 25,000 EVs by the year 2015. GE's chief executive, <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Immelt" title="Jeffrey Immelt" class="mw-redirect">Jeffrey Immelt</a>, said that specifically, the company would convert half of its corporate fleet vehicles to EVs by the year 2015 in an effort to give the new technology a jump start along with helping to develop a potentially big new consumer market for the vehicles. GE told the media that by electrifying its own fleet, the company will accelerate the adoption curve, drive scale, and move of EVs from anticipation to action. The company had originally hinted at this plan in late September.</p>
<p>The details of the announcement were that GE said it will buy 12,000 GM vehicles starting next year, beginning with the <a href="/wiki/Chevy_Volt" title="Chevy Volt" class="mw-redirect">Chevy Volt</a>. GE also plans to add other different types of EVs as a variety of automakers expand their electric car offerings and more cars come to the market. Every major automaker has plans to introduce cars that can be powered by electricity over the next two years. In addition, GE is hoping that its planned purchase will help drive down costs by increasing production volumes and assuring automakers that they will have at least one big buyer in the near future.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Future">Future</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: Future">edit source</a><span class="mw-editsection-divider"> | </span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Electric_vehicle&amp;veaction=edit&amp;section=59" title="Edit section: Future" class="mw-editsection-visualeditor">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Battery_electric_vehicle" title="Battery electric vehicle">Battery electric vehicle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid" title="Plug-in hybrid">Plug-in hybrid</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
